Imam's Hadath and the Sole Follower: A Hanafi Dispute
Explaining the ruling when imam has hadath and sole follower is a woman or child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling when an imam has hadath and the sole follower is a woman or child, and explain why the imam's prayer remains valid.

Core Idea
When the imam leaves, the follower is specified; if unfit, the imam's prayer stays valid.

This lesson explores a nuanced scenario in congregational prayer: the imam leading one person experiences hadath and leaves the mosque without appointing a successor. The matn states that if the sole follower is a man, he automatically becomes imam. But if the follower is a woman or child, a dispute arises. We will examine the two Hanafi opinions and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand the situation: imam with one follower has hadath and leaves without istikhlaf. The follower may be a man, woman, or child.
The scenario is from 'Umda al-Ri'aya commentary on Al-Wiqaya.
2
State the Two Opinions
One opinion says the imam's prayer becomes invalid when the sole follower is a woman/child; the other says it remains valid. Identify each.
Both opinions are transmitted within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the Basis of Each Opinion
The invalidating opinion likens the situation to an implicit appointment of an unfit person. The validating opinion argues that the imam did nothing actively; the transfer occurred without his action.
The key difference is whether the imam's inaction is imputed as a cause.
4
Identify the Relied-Upon Opinion and Its Proof
The more correct (al-ashah) view is that the imam's prayer is valid, supported by Al-Kafi, Qadhi Khan, and Al-Tamartashi. The proof: imamate transferred without the imam's action.
This is the mufta bihi position for fatwa.
5
Apply the Ruling to Practice
In practice, if the imam leaves and the sole follower is a woman, the imam's prayer is valid. However, the follower's prayer may be invalid if she remains without an imam (since she cannot lead a man).
The follower should intend to pray alone or follow a qualified imam if possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن أم واحداً فأحدث ، فالرجلُ إمام بلا نية إن كان وإلا قيل : تفسد صلاته) : أي إن أم واحداً فأحدث الإمام، فإن كان المؤتم رجلاً يصير إماماً " . غير أن ينوي الإمام إمامته ؛ لأنَّ النِّيَّةَ التَّعيين ، وهنا هو متعين، وإن كان امرأة، أو صبياً، قيل : تفسد صلاة الإمام ؛ لأنَّ المرأة أو الصبي صارَ إماماً له لتعينه ، وقيل : لا تفسد ؛ لأنه لم يوجد منه الاستخلاف، وفي صورة الرجل إنما يصير إماماً ؛ لتعينه وصلاحيته، وهاهنا لم يصلُح ، فلم يصر إماماً، والإمام إمام كما كان، لكن المقتدي بقي بلا إمام فتفسد صلاته. قوله : وإن أمّ واحداً .. الخ ؛ يعني إذا صلّى رجل إماماً برجل واحد، وأحدث الإمام فذهب للتوضؤ وخرج من المسجد من غير استخلاف، فالمقتدي إمام له من غير حاجة إلى نية الإمام إمامته ، ولا إلى نية ذلك المقتدى ، فيتم الأول صلاته خلفه. قوله : فالرجل ؛ الأولى أن يقول : فهو ، أو قالوا حد أو نحو ذلك، أو يحذف قيد إن كان رجلاً. قوله : إماماً ؛ لما فيه من صيانة صلاته، فإنّه لو لم يتعين إماماً لخلى موضعُ الإمامة عن الإمام، وهو موجب لفسادِ صلاة المقتدي. قوله : للتعين ؛ فإن المقتدي إذا كان غير واحد يحتاج إلى استخلاف واحد منهم ؛ لقطع المزاحمة، فإذا توحد صار التعين موجوداً حكماً. قوله : لأن المرأة أو الصبي...الخ ؛ يعني أنه إذا تعين الصبي والمرأة للإمامة ؛ لعدم المزاحم، فكأنه وُجِدَ منه استخلافُ مَن لا يصلح للإمامة، وهو مفسد للصلاة. قوله : وقيل لا تفسد ؛ هذا هو الأصح ، كما في «الكافي»، وقال قاضي خان في «شرح الجامع الصغير»: هو الصحيح، وقال التمرتاشي: الأصح أنَّ صلاة الإمام لا تفسد ؛ لأن الإمامة انتقلت من غير صنعه.
If he leads one person and then had an impurity, then the man is an imam without intention if he (the follower) is (a man), and if not, it is said: his prayer becomes invalid. Meaning: if he leads one person and the imam has an impurity, then if the follower is a man, he becomes an imam without the imam intending his imamate, because intention is for specification, and here he is specified. And if it is a woman or a child, it is said: the imam's prayer becomes invalid because the woman or child becomes the imam for him due to his specification. And it is said: it does not become invalid because no delegation occurred from him. In the case of a man, he becomes imam due to his specification and qualification, but here (the woman/child) is not qualified, so he does not become imam, and the imam remains imam as before, but the follower remains without an imam, so his prayer becomes invalid. His statement: 'If he leads one person, etc.' meaning: if a man prays as imam with one man, and the imam has an impurity and goes to perform wudu and leaves the mosque without delegating, then the follower is an imam for him without need for the imam's intention of his imamate, nor the follower's intention, so the first completes his prayer behind him. His statement: 'the man' – it would be better to say 'he', or it is said 'limit' or similar, or remove the condition 'if he is a man'. His statement: 'an imam' – because of preserving his prayer, for if he did not become specified as imam, the place of imamate would be empty of an imam, which causes invalidation of the follower's prayer. His statement: 'for specification' – if the followers are more than one, there is need to delegate one of them to remove competition, so when there is only one, specification is present effectively. His statement: 'because the woman or the child, etc.' meaning: if the child and woman become specified for imamate due to lack of competition, it is as if he has delegated someone unfit for imamate, which invalidates the prayer. His statement: 'and it is said: it does not invalidate' – this is the more correct, as in Al-Kafi, and Qadhi Khan said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: it is the correct one, and Al-Tamartashi said: the more correct is that the imam's prayer does not invalidate because the imamate transferred without his action.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a scenario where the imam leading one person experiences hadath (ritual impurity) and leaves the prayer without appointing a successor. The matn states that if the sole follower is a man, he becomes the imam without needing any intention from the original imam, because the follower is the only one, so he is specified (muta'ayyin). If the follower is a woman or a child, there is a difference of opinion: one view says the prayer becomes invalid, another says it does not. The commentator explains that the first view likens the situation to an implicit appointment of someone unfit, while the second view—which is considered the more correct (al-ashah) according to Al-Kafi, Qadhi Khan, and Al-Tamartashi—argues that since the imam did not actively appoint the woman or child, the imamate transferred without his action, so his prayer remains valid. However, the follower's prayer may become invalid if he remains without an imam.

Key Terms
استخلاف (istikhlāf): The act of the imam appointing a successor to lead the prayer when he is unable to continue.
تعين (ta‘ayyun): Specification: the follower becomes automatically the imam when there is no other qualified follower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The imam's prayer becomes invalid.
B) The imam's prayer remains valid.
C) The imam's prayer is complete but makruh.
D) The imam's prayer is invalid because he left the mosque.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, if an imam with a sole follower leaves due to hadath without istikhlaf, the follower becomes imam by specification; if the follower is a woman or child, the imam's prayer remains valid per the relied-upon view.

Speech Invalidates Prayer Even if Forgetful
Sharah opening chapter on invalidators: ruling on speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that speech invalidates prayer, identify the hadith proof, and distinguish between sahw, nisyan, and khata regarding their effect.

Core Idea
Any speech in prayer invalidates it, whether intentional, forgetful, or mistaken.

This passage opens the chapter on invalidators of prayer after discussing involuntary interruptions. The key ruling is that speech invalidates prayer, even if done forgetfully. The proof is a sahih hadith that explicitly restricts prayer to tasbih, takbir, and Quran. We will explore the generality of the proof and the types of forgetfulness that still render the prayer invalid.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its basis
Identify that the ruling is: speech invalidates prayer. The basis is the hadith's general negation indicating that no speech is permitted.
2
Examine the hadith proof
Read the hadith: 'Indeed this prayer of ours is not suitable for any speech of people; it is only tasbih, takbir, and recitation of Quran.' Understand that this is an ahad hadith, but sufficient for obligation.
3
Understand the generality
The words 'kalam' and the general negation imply that all speech, little or much, invalidates. Even two letters or one meaningful letter count as speech.
4
Consider forgetfulness
The text explicitly says 'even if forgetfully'. This includes sahw (easily recalled), nisyan (completely forgotten), and khata (mistake). All invalidate.
5
Apply the exception
A single meaningless letter or a mere sound does not invalidate. Only meaningful speech (whether one letter if it conveys meaning, or two letters) invalidates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها ( يفسدها الكلام ولو سهواً ) قوله : باب ما يفسد ... الخ ؛ لما كانت العوارض العارضة في الصلاة على قسمين : اختيارية واضطرارية، وكانت الاضطرارية هي الأصل في العروض، وأحق بالتقديم، قدم ذكر الحدث في الصلاة وما يتعلّق به، ثم عقبه بذكر الاختيارية، وهي إما أن تكون مفسدة للصلاة، أو تجعلها مكروهة، فذكرهما في باب واحد، فقدم الأول عنواناً وبياناً ؛ لكونه أقوى أثراً. والفساد والبطلان في العبادات بمعنى واحد ، وهو خروج العبادة عن كونها عبادة بسبب فوت بعض الفرائض من الشرائط والأركان، وما يفوت الوصف فيه مع بقاء الأصل يسمى مكروهاً. قوله : يفسدها ؛ أي الصلاة، وكذا سجدة التلاوة والسهو والشكر ؛ لكونها في حكم الصلاة. قوله : يفسدها الكلام ؛ الأصل فيه قوله : «إنّ صلاتنا هذه لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس، وإنّما هي التسبيح والتكبير وقراءة القرآن)، رواه مسلم في صحيحه»، وأبو داود والطبراني وغيرهم، وفهم من إطلاق الكلام والنفي العام أنّ الكلام مطلقاً مفسد ، قليلاً كان أو كثيراً، فيفسد النطق بحرفين أو حرف مفهم : كع وَقِ أمْراً ، ولا يفسد الحرف الواحد المهمل ، ولا الصوتُ المجرّد. كذا في «البحر»(۳)، وغيره. قوله : ولو سهواً ؛ وصلية ؛ أي ولو كان الكلام في الصلاة سهواً، وكذا إذا كان نسياناً أو خطأ ، والفرق بين هذه الثلاثة : أن السهو ما يتبنه فيه بأدنى التنبه. والنسيان : أن يخرج المدركُ من الخيال أيضاً، فيحتاج إلى إدراك جديد، وتنبيه قوي.
Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. Speech invalidates it, even if forgetfully. His statement: 'Chapter on what invalidates...' etc. Because the incidental occurrences that happen in prayer are of two types: voluntary and involuntary. The involuntary type was the original in occurrence and more deserving of precedence, so he first mentioned hadath in prayer and what relates to it, then followed it with mention of the voluntary type. The voluntary type either invalidates the prayer or makes it disliked, so he mentioned both in one chapter, giving precedence to the first in title and explanation because it is stronger in effect. Fasad and butlan in acts of worship have the same meaning, which is the act of worship leaving the state of being an act of worship due to the loss of some obligations from the conditions and pillars. What loses the description while the essence remains is called makruh. His statement: 'invalidates it' – meaning the prayer, and likewise the prostration of recitation, forgetfulness, and thanks because they are in the ruling of prayer. His statement: 'speech invalidates it' – the basis for it is his statement, peace be upon him: 'Indeed this prayer of ours is not suitable for any speech of people; it is only tasbih, takbir, and recitation of the Quran.' Related by Muslim in his Sahih, and Abu Dawud and al-Tabarani and others. It is understood from the generality of the words 'speech' and the general negation that speech absolutely invalidates, whether little or much. So uttering two letters or one meaningful letter like 'ع' or 'ق' as a command invalidates, but a single meaningless letter does not invalidate, nor does a mere sound. Thus in Al-Bahr and others. His statement: 'even if forgetfully' – is a condition; meaning even if the speech in prayer is forgetful, and likewise if it is due to forgetfulness or mistake. The difference between these three: sahw is that which is perceived with the slightest attention. Nisyan is that the perception leaves the imagination as well, requiring new perception and strong attention.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on invalidators and disliked matters in prayer. The commentator first explains the structure: involuntary interruptions (like hadath) were covered before, now voluntary ones are addressed. Fasad and butlan are synonymous in worship—the act ceases to be a valid act of worship due to missing a pillar or condition. If only a recommended aspect is missed, it is makruh. The main ruling: speech invalidates prayer, even if done forgetfully (sahwan). The proof is the hadith reported by Muslim: 'This prayer of ours is not fit for any speech of people; it is only tasbih, takbir, and recitation of Quran.' The general prohibition indicates that any speech—even two letters or one meaningful letter—invalidates, but a single meaningless sound does not. Three types of forgetfulness are distinguished: sahw (easily recalled with slight attention), nisyan (completely forgotten, requiring new recollection), and khata (mistake). All invalidate prayer if they involve speech.

Key Terms
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness that can be easily recalled with slight attention; in this passage, it invalidates prayer if it involves speech.
النسيان (al-nisyān): Complete forgetfulness where the memory is gone and requires new recollection; also invalidates prayer if speech occurs.
الفساد والبطلان (al-fasād wa al-buṭlān): In the context of worship, both terms are synonymous and mean the act loses its validity due to missing a pillar or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between sahw and nisyan?
A) Sahw is recalled with slight attention; nisyan requires new perception.
B) Sahw is intentional; nisyan is unintentional.
C) Sahw does not invalidate; nisyan invalidates.
D) Sahw applies to speech; nisyan applies to actions.

Key Takeaway
Speech invalidates prayer regardless of intentionality or forgetfulness, based on the hadith's absolute prohibition of worldly speech in prayer.

Salah — Prayer

The Ruling of Speech in Prayer: Invalidating Even if Forgetful
Ruling on invalidation by speech with proofs and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that any human speech in prayer invalidates it, even if forgetful, sleepy, or under duress, and explain the exception for the greeting (salam) when forgetful and seated.

Core Idea
Speech in prayer invalidates absolutely — only the greeting (salam) when forgetful and seated is an exception.

In this passage, the commentator of al-Wiqāyah explains the foundational ruling on what nullifies prayer. The key invalidation is speech — any human utterance that is not part of the prayer. But the ruling is not without nuance: forgetfulness, sleep, and even the greeting (salām) are examined. By understanding the distinction between different types of speech and states, you will grasp the Hanafi position with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Speech (kalām) in prayer. The ruling: it invalidates (yufsid) the prayer. This is the general rule.
The term 'yufsid' here means the prayer becomes invalid and must be repeated.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith: 'This prayer of ours is not suitable for any speech of people...' (Muslim). Understand that the general negation indicates total prohibition and invalidation.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning (dalāla) is clear and decisive for the Hanafis.
3
Understand the scope: absolute invalidation
The passage clarifies that even if speech is forgetful (sahw), due to nisyān, khaṭa', or even in sleep, the prayer is invalid. Only pure sound or a single meaningless letter is exempt. Also includes ignorance and duress.
This is the mukhtār (chosen) view; some early Hanafis exempted sleep, but the later relied-upon position rejects that.
4
Analyze the special case of salam
The greeting (salām) is treated differently because it is both speech (kalām) and remembrance (dhikr). The passage distinguishes: salām as salutation (taḥiyya) invalidates absolutely; salām to exit prayer (taḥlīl) only invalidates if intentional and in a seated position (or while standing in non-funeral prayer). If forgetful with assumption of completion, it does not invalidate.
This reconciliation (from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq) resolves apparent contradictions in earlier texts.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and mufta bihi
There is disagreement on sleep and on the conditions of salam. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that speech invalidates absolutely, even in sleep, and salam invalidates as described. The tarjīh is based on the strength of the hadith and the principle of incompatibility between prayer and speech.
Authority: al-Bahr al-Rā'iq and al-Durr al-Mukhtār.

Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. Speech invalidates it, even if forgetfully. [1] Chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. (Speech invalidates it, even if forgetfully (4)). [1] His saying: 'Chapter on what invalidates...' etc.; since the incidental occurrences in prayer are of two types: voluntary and involuntary, and involuntary occurrences are the original in occurrence and more deserving of precedence, he presented the mention of hadath (ritual impurity) in prayer and what relates to it, then followed it with the mention of voluntary occurrences, which are either invalidating for prayer or make it disliked, so he mentioned both in one chapter, giving precedence to the first as a title and explanation because it is stronger in effect. And fasād and bātil in acts of worship are synonymous, meaning the act's exiting from being an act of worship due to the loss of some obligations of conditions and pillars, and what loses its perfection while the essence remains is called makrūh. [2] His saying: 'Invalidates it' i.e., the prayer, and likewise the prostration of recitation, forgetfulness, and gratitude because they are in the ruling of prayer. (3) His saying: 'Speech invalidates it'; the basis for it is his saying: 'Indeed this prayer of ours is not suitable for any speech of people; rather it is glorification, takbīr, and recitation of the Quran,' narrated by Muslim in his Sahih, Abu Dawud, al-Tabarani, and others. It is understood from the absolute (unqualified) mention of speech and the general negation that speech absolutely invalidates, whether little or much. So uttering two letters or one meaningful letter like 'qif' (stop) as a command invalidates, but a single meaningless letter does not invalidate, nor does pure sound. As stated in al-Bahr and other works. [4] His saying: 'even if forgetfully' i.e., even if the speech in prayer was forgetfully, and likewise if it was due to nisyān (forgetting) or khata' (error). Nisyān (forgetting) is that the perception exits from the imagination as well, requiring new perception and strong attention. ... Or in sleep, and the greeting (salām) intentionally. [1] Or in sleep, and the greeting intentionally, restricted by intentionality because the greeting forgetfully is not invalidating because it is among the remembrances (adhkār). So in non-intentional cases, it is considered remembrance, and in intentional cases, it is considered speech. The form of error (khaṭa') is that one intends to recite or glorify or the like, but human speech flows on his tongue. (1) [1] His saying: 'in sleep' i.e., he slept in prayer in a manner that does not nullify wuḍū', then spoke therein. This is the chosen view contrary to some of our elders, who said that prayer is not invalidated by speaking while asleep, based on the fact that there is no negligence in sleep, as reported in the hadith in Sunan Abi Dawud and elsewhere. The view of the chosen opinion is that the absence of negligence only affects the removal of sin, not the absence of invalidation, for its basis is the incompatibility between prayer and speech as indicated by the hadith: 'No speech of people is suitable in it.' So it invalidates absolutely; hence, if one spoke ignorantly or under duress, it also invalidates, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtār and other works. [2] His saying: 'and the greeting intentionally' i.e., deliberately. Their expressions differ in the 'Chapter on Greeting': among them are those who restricted invalidation by it to intentional; among them are those who made it absolute (general). And reconciliation between them was made in al-Bahr: that the greeting of salutation (taḥiyya) invalidates absolutely, whether intentional or forgetful, even if he did not say 'alaykum', because it is speech and address, so intentional and unintentional are equal. And the greeting of tahlīl (exiting the prayer) i.e., for exiting the prayer before its completion, if intentional, it invalidates; if forgetful or with the assumption of completion, it does not invalidate if he is in a seated position; if he is standing in prayer other than the funeral prayer, it invalidates because standing is not a proper occasion for salutation. [3] His saying: 'Because the greeting...' etc.; its gist is that the greeting is speech in one respect, remembrance in another, for 'peace' is one of the names of Allah Almighty. (1) From Abu Qatada, he said: 'Indeed there is no negligence in sleep. Negligence is only on the one who does not pray the prayer until the time of the next prayer comes.' In Sahih Muslim, Sahih Ibn Khuzayma, etc. (2) al-Durr al-Mukhtār (1:614-615). (3) al-Bahr al-Rā'iq (2:8).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter heading and the first ruling of the chapter on what invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. The commentator begins by explaining why the author grouped invalidating acts and disliked acts in one chapter: the incidental occurrences in prayer are of two types, involuntary (like hadath) and voluntary. Since involuntary ones are more foundational, they were given precedence; the voluntary ones are either invalidating or disliked. The commentator then clarifies that fasād and buṭlān are synonymous in acts of worship, meaning the act ceases to be valid due to loss of a pillar or condition, while loss of perfection without loss of essence is termed makrūh (disliked). The key ruling is that speech invalidates prayer, even if done forgetfully (sahwan). The proof is the famous hadith: 'This prayer of ours is not suitable for any speech of people; it is only glorification, takbīr, and recitation of Quran.' (Muslim). Since the hadith is general, any speech—little or much—invalidates. Details: uttering two letters or one meaningful letter (like 'qif' meaning 'stop') invalidates; one meaningless letter does not; pure sound does not. The commentator distinguishes between sahw (forgetfulness) and nisyān (forgetting) and khaṭa' (error). Sahw is what can be realized with minimal attention; nisyān requires new perception; khaṭa' is when one intends dhikr but misses. Regarding sleep, the chosen view (mukhtār) is that speech in sleep invalidates because the ruling is based on incompatibility between prayer and speech, not on negligence. The same applies if one spoke ignorantly or under duress. As for the greeting (salām), the commentator distinguishes: if it is meant as a salutation (tahiyya), it invalidates whether intentional or forgetful because it is speech; if it is the salutation of exiting prayer (salām al-taḥlīl) before completion, it invalidates only if done intentionally and the person is in the seated position. If done forgetfully or with the assumption that prayer is complete, it does not invalidate. Standing is not a position for salutation, so if one says salām while standing, it invalidates. The reason salām can be considered remembrance is that 'salām' is a name of Allah. However, the intentional salām is treated as invalidating speech.

Key Terms
الكلام (al-kalām): Speech; any utterance of at least two letters or one meaningful letter that conveys meaning to others, which invalidates prayer.
السهو (al-sahw): Forgetfulness during prayer; the state of being unaware of one's actions with the ability to realize easily; speech in this state invalidates.
السلام (al-salām): The greeting of 'assalamu alaykum' during prayer. It is considered invalidating speech if done intentionally to exit prayer or as salutation, except when forgetful and in seated position with assumption of completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Speech invalidates prayer absolutely, with only a narrow exception for the greeting (salām) when forgetful and seated assuming completion.

Ruling on Returning the Greeting During Prayer
Clarifying the ruling on returning salam in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning the greeting (radd al-salam) during prayer and identify the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
Verbal greeting invalidates prayer; hand gesture does not.

You know that speaking during prayer invalidates it, but what about returning the greeting? Some think it is permissible because it is a short phrase, but the Hanafi school holds it invalidates the prayer even if unintentional. In this lesson, we will examine the evidence from hadith and the distinction between verbal and gestured responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is returning the greeting (radd al-salam) during salah. The Hanafi ruling is that it invalidates the prayer if done verbally.
The word 'رد' in the matn is left unrestricted to include both intentional and unintentional.
2
Determine the hukm level
It is haram because it invalidates the prayer, and speech is prohibited. The hukm level is based on the hadith prohibiting speech.
Some earlier scholars considered unintentional as dhikr, but the relied-upon view is that it invalidates.
3
Locate the dalil
The main dalil is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet said 'Indeed in prayer there is occupation' (إن في الصلاة شغلاً). Also a narration prohibits speaking in prayer.
The hadith is ahad but accepted as evidence.
4
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'occupation' indicates that one should be occupied only with the prayer, thus any speech, including returning a greeting, is forbidden and invalidates.
This applies to all speech, not just returning the greeting.
5
Note the exception for gesture
Returning the greeting with the hand (ishara) does not invalidate, as established by the Prophet's practice.
This is based on another hadith where the Prophet gestured in response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ورده) ، لم يقيدِ الرَّدَّ بالعمد، ويخطر ببالي أنه إنما أطلق ؛ لأنه مفسد عمداً كان، أو سهواً ؛ لأن رد السلام ليس من الأذكار، بـل هـو كـلام، وتخاطب، والكلامُ مُفْسِدٌ عَمَداً كان أَو سَهْواً. وقد شرع السلام في التشهد أيضاً، وكونه كلاماً باعتبار كونه خطاباً، وإنما يتحقق حكمـه عنـد الـقـصـد ولا قصد في السهو والنسيان، ففي العـمـد يجعـل كـلامـاً ؛ لكونه خطاباً، ويحكم بكونه مفسداً، وفي غيره يعتبر شبه الذكر، فلا يحكم بكونه مفسداً. وظاهر كلام الشارح هاهنا يقتضي أن لا يفسد سلام التحية أيضاً في غير العمد، وقد عرفت أن التحقيق خلاف ذلك، فلا بد أن يقيد كلام الماتن والشارح بسلام التحليل، ويقال : سلام التحيّة لما كان على الغير ترجَّحت فيه جهة الكلاميّة، فلا يعتبر شبه الذكر فيه. [1] قوله : ورده ؛ أي جواب سلام التحيّة إن كان بلسانه، وأما بيده فليس بمفسد، كما في «البحر» و«الحلية»، وقد ثبت عن ابن مسعود له : «كنا نسلم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في الصلاة فيرد علينا، فلما رجعنا من عند النجاشي ملك الحبشة سلمنا عليه فلم يردّ علينا، وقال : إنّ في الصلاة شغلاً) (۱) وفي رواية: «إنَّ الله يحدث من أمره ما يشاء، وقد أحدث أن لا تكلّموا في الصلاة ) ، أخرجه أبو داود وابن حبان، وغيرهما، وثبت في السنن أنه أجاب السلام بيده في الصلاة(۳).
And returning the greeting: He did not qualify the returning with intent, and it occurs to my mind that he left it unrestricted because it invalidates whether intentional or unintentional, because returning the greeting is not among the remembrances (adhkar), but rather it is speech and address, and speech invalidates whether intentional or unintentional. And peace (salam) has also been prescribed in the tashahhud, and it being speech is by virtue of it being address, and its ruling is only realized at the time of intent, and there is no intent in forgetfulness and inadvertence. So in the case of intent, it is considered speech because it is address, and it is ruled to be an invalidator; otherwise it is considered resembling remembrance, so it is not ruled to be an invalidator. The apparent statement of the commentator here implies that the greeting of tahiyya also does not invalidate in the case of non-intent, but you have known that the verified position is contrary to that. So it is necessary to qualify the statement of the author and commentator to the greeting of tahlil (the final salam of the prayer). And it is said: The greeting of tahiyya, because it is directed to another person, has the aspect of speech dominant, so resemblance to remembrance is not considered in it. [1] His statement: 'and returning the greeting' means the answer to the greeting of tahiyya if it is with his tongue. As for with his hand, it does not invalidate, as in 'al-Bahr' and 'al-Hilya'. And it has been established from Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him): 'We used to greet the Prophet (peace be upon him) during prayer and he would respond to us. When we returned from the Negus, the king of Abyssinia, we greeted him and he did not respond to us, and said: "Indeed, in prayer there is occupation."' And in another narration: 'Indeed, Allah creates from His command what He wills, and He has introduced that you should not speak during prayer.' Recorded by Abu Dawud and Ibn Hibban and others. And it is established in the Sunan that he responded to the greeting with his hand during prayer.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of returning the greeting (radd al-salam) during prayer. The author of the commentary first explains that the word 'رد' in the base text is left unrestricted because returning the greeting invalidates the prayer whether done intentionally or unintentionally. This is because returning the greeting is not like the remembrances (adhkar) such as tasbih or takbir; it is actual speech addressed to another person, and speech invalidates the prayer regardless of intent. However, the commentary then notes a nuance: the greeting (salam) is also prescribed in the tashahhud during the prayer, but that is considered dhikr because it is addressed to Allah and the angels, not to another person. For a greeting directed to another person (salam al-tahiyya), if done intentionally, it is speech and invalidates; if done unintentionally (forgetfully), some scholars considered it like dhikr and thus not invalidating. But the commentary clarifies that the correct view is that even unintentionally, greeting another person invalidates because the speech aspect is dominant. Therefore, the statement of the author and commentator should be qualified to the final salam (salam al-tahlil) which is the obligatory exit from prayer, not the greeting of tahiyya. Additionally, returning the greeting with the hand (gesture) does not invalidate the prayer, as established by the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud and the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) who gestured to response while in prayer. The hadith evidence shows that speaking during prayer was initially allowed but later abrogated when the Prophet said, 'There is occupation in prayer.' This ruling is based on the general principle that speech invalidates prayer, and returning the greeting falls under speech.

Key Terms
رد السلام (radd al-salam): Responding to a greeting of 'as-salamu alaykum' by saying 'wa alaykum as-salam' or similar.
سلام التحية (salam al-tahiyya): The greeting directed to another person, as opposed to the final salam of the prayer (salam al-tahlil).
سلام التحليل (salam al-tahlil): The final greeting(s) at the end of prayer that exit the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what is the ruling on returning a greeting (radd al-salam) during prayer with the tongue?
A) It invalidates the prayer whether intentional or unintentional.
B) It invalidates only if intentional.
C) It does not invalidate because it is dhikr.
D) It invalidates only if spoken loudly.

Key Takeaway
Returning a verbal greeting during prayer invalidates it; a gesture does not.

Ruling on Groaning, Saying 'Ah' and 'Uff' During Prayer
Explaining that groaning and similar sounds invalidate prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on groaning and similar sounds in prayer and explain the legal reasoning (wajh) for their invalidation.

Core Idea
Groaning or saying 'ah'/'uff' during prayer invalidates it because these sounds produce letters and are considered speech.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines three types of vocalizations that occur during prayer: groaning (anin), saying 'awh' (ta'wuh), and saying 'uff' (ta'fif). The commentator explains that these are treated as speech (kalam) because they consist of letters, and any intentional speech invalidates prayer. In this lesson, we will examine the definition of these terms and the rational proof for the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What sounds are being discussed? What is the ruling (invalid)?
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is that these are forms of kalam (speech).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
They produce letters, thus are speech.
4
Note conditions
This applies to sounds that produce letters; coughing or sneezing are excluded.
5
Consider ikhtilaf
Some schools may differentiate if involuntary, but Hanafi holds even if from pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2] قوله : والأنين ; هذا وما بعده معطوف على قوله : «الكلام» كقوله : «السلام»، و«رده»، والأنين على وزن فعيل ، عبارة مما يخرج من فم المتوجع ، والمتحزن، كأن يقول : آه بالقصر. والتأوه : بتشديد الواو على وزن التفعّل أن يقول : أوه، أو آه بالمد. والتأفيف: هو أن يقول : أف أو تف. والوجه في فساد الصلاة بهذه الأشياء، وكذا بالبكاء، فإنّ المراد به ما يخرح به حروف، كونه كلاماً وهو مطلقاً مفسد.
[2] His statement: 'and groaning' (al-anin). This and what follows are conjuncts to his statement: 'speech' (al-kalam), like his statement: 'greeting' and 'returning it'. Al-anin is on the pattern of fa'il, an expression for what issues from the mouth of one in pain or sorrow, like saying 'ah' (ā) with shortened vowel. And al-ta'wuh: with shaddah on the waw, on the pattern of tafa''ul, meaning he says 'awh' or 'āh' with elongation. And al-ta'fif: he says 'uff' or 'taff'. The reasoning for the invalidity of prayer with these things, and similarly with crying, is that what is intended is that which produces letters, it being speech, and it is absolutely an invalidator.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'anin' (groaning), 'ta'wuh' (saying 'awh' or 'ah'), and 'ta'fif' (saying 'uff' or 'taff'). These are sounds that come out from a person in pain or sorrow. The commentator explains that these are considered speech (kalam) because they consist of letters (huroof) that convey meaning. The underlying principle is that any act that produces letters (at least two letters, as will be clarified later) is considered speech and invalidates the prayer. Therefore, groaning, moaning, or uttering expressions of pain during prayer invalidates it, regardless of the person's condition. This ruling is based on the general rule that speech invalidates prayer, and these utterances fall under speech because they consist of meaningful letters. The commentary also mentions that crying (buka') will be discussed similarly.

Key Terms
الأنين (al-anin): Sounds of groaning from pain, like 'ah' (قصر).
التأوه (al-ta'wuh): Saying 'awh' or 'ah' with elongation.
التأفيف (al-ta'fif): Saying 'uff' or 'taff'.

Key Takeaway
Groaning and similar sounds invalidate prayer because they constitute speech (kalam) by producing letters.

Ruling on Crying During Prayer
Distinguishing crying that invalidates prayer from crying that does not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for crying that invalidates prayer and distinguish between valid and invalidating crying based on source and articulation.

Core Idea
Crying from Allah-fear is worship; crying from worldly pain with letters breaks prayer.

Crying during prayer is a common occurrence, but its legal effect depends on the cause and form. This passage from a Hanafi sharh clarifies when crying invalidates prayer and when it is excused. Understanding these nuances ensures your prayer remains valid and you avoid unnecessary repetition.

Guided Path
1
Define crying (buka') linguistically
Distinguish between 'buka' with elongation (sound+tears) and with shortening (tears only). The matn's qualifier 'with sound' restricts the ruling to the first meaning.
The second meaning (tears without sound) never invalidates.
2
Identify the conditions for invalidation
Two conditions: (1) the crying produces two or more letters (articulation), (2) the cause is worldly pain (body) or calamity (wealth).
Tears without sound or sound without letters are not invalidating.
3
Recognize the exceptions
Crying from fear of Allah (remembrance of Jannah/Nar) does not invalidate, even with letters. Involuntary sounds (sick person's moan, sneeze, cough) are also excused.
The rationale is that these are not intentional speech.
4
Apply the underlying principle
The ruling is based on qiyas to other uncontrollable acts (sneezing, coughing). Intentional speech invalidates prayer; what is beyond control is excused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1] قوله : وبكاء ; هو بالضم وبالمد : صوت مع خروج الدمع، وبالقصر : خروج الدمع. كذا في «الصحاح». فقوله : «بصوت» للتقييد على الثاني، وللتوضيح على الأول. [2]قوله : بصوت ; أي بشرط أن يحصل به حرفان فصاعداً، أما خروج الدمع بلا صوت أو صوت لا حروف معه فغير مفسد. كذا في «النهر»، و«الفتح»، و«النهاية». (۳) قوله : من وجع ; أي في بدنه. ومصيبة ; أي في ماله. وهذا قيد للبكاء احترازاً عمّا إذا كان البكاء من ذكر الجنة والنار، فإنّه لا يفسد كما سيذكره ، وقيل : هو متعلّق بالأربعة، ويستثنى منه مريض لا يملك نفسه عن أنين أو تأوه ، فإنّه لا يفسد ; لأنه حينئذ كعطاس وسعال وجثاء وتثاؤب ; فإنّها لا تفسد وإن حصل بها حروف; لكون ما لا اختيار فيه معفواً.
[1] His statement: 'and crying' (buka'). It is with dammah and elongation: sound with shedding of tears, and with qasr (shortening): just the shedding of tears. Thus in 'al-Sihah'. So his statement 'with sound' is a qualifier for the second meaning, and explanatory for the first. [2] His statement: 'with sound' means on condition that two or more letters are produced. As for the shedding of tears without sound or a sound without letters, it is not an invalidator. Thus in 'al-Nahr', 'al-Fath', and 'al-Nihayah'. (3) His statement: 'from pain' means in his body. 'Or calamity' means in his wealth. This is a qualification for crying to exclude when the crying is from the remembrance of Paradise and Hellfire, for it does not invalidate, as he will mention. And it is said: it applies to all four (i.e., anin, ta'wuh, ta'fif, and buka'), and an exception is made for the sick person who cannot control himself from groaning or moaning, for it does not invalidate because it is like sneezing, coughing, spitting, and yawning; these do not invalidate even if letters occur because that which is not under one's control is excused.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage focuses on crying (buka') during prayer. The commentator first clarifies the linguistic meaning: 'buka'' with a long vowel means sound with tears, while with a short vowel it means simply tears. The matn includes the qualifier 'with sound' (bisaot), which is explained as meaning that the crying must produce two or more letters (huroof) to invalidate the prayer. If tears come out silently or a sound without letters (like a scream without articulation) is made, it does not invalidate. Then, the commentator adds another condition: the crying must be due to pain in the body or calamity in wealth. If the crying is from the remembrance of Paradise and Hellfire (fear of Allah or longing for Jannah), it does not invalidate the prayer. This is because such crying is considered an act of devotion and not mere speech. Additionally, an exception is made for a sick person who cannot control his groaning or moaning; such involuntary sounds are excused like coughing or sneezing, even if they produce letters, because they are not under his control. This ruling is based on the general principle that intentional speech invalidates, but what is unavoidable is excused.

Key Terms
البكاء (al-buka'): Crying: can mean sound with tears (with long vowel) or just tears (with short vowel). In this context, it means crying with sound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does crying (buka') during prayer NOT invalidate the prayer according to this passage?
A) When it produces two letters.
B) When it is due to pain in the body.
C) When it is from the remembrance of Paradise and Hellfire.
D) When it is loud.

Key Takeaway
Crying invalidates prayer only when it is intentional, articulated with letters, and stemming from worldly causes; spiritual or involuntary crying is excused.

When Does Clearing the Throat Invalidate Prayer?
Ruling with proof and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on tanahnuh during prayer and IDENTIFY which conditions make it an invalidator and which exceptions preserve validity.

Core Idea
Clearing the throat invalidates prayer only if voluntary and not for a prayer benefit.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the specific ruling on clearing the throat (tanahnuh) during prayer. While it is generally considered speech and thus an invalidator, the Hanafi school provides important exceptions when the action is involuntary or serves a legitimate prayer purpose. Understanding these conditions is crucial for maintaining the validity of one's prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The passage defines tanahnuh as saying 'ah, ah' (producing letters). The default ruling is that it invalidates the prayer because it is considered speech (kalam).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The reason (wajh) for invalidation is that it constitutes speech. In Hanafi fiqh, intentional speech invalidates prayer based on authentic hadith.
3
Extract the two conditions for invalidation
The passage states two conditions must both be met for tanahnuh to invalidate: (1) it is done without a valid excuse (i.e., not involuntary), and (2) it is not for a valid purpose related to the prayer.
4
Note the exceptions
Three exceptions are explicitly mentioned: involuntary throat clearing due to natural disposition, clearing the throat to improve recitation, to inform others of being in prayer, or to guide the imam to the correct recitation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage concludes with 'wa hadha kullu huwa al-sahih' (all this is the correct view), indicating the relied-upon position in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤]قوله : وتنحنح ; هو أن يقول : أح أح بالفتح والضم، قاله في البحر»). ووجه الفساد فيه : كونه كلاماً، ويشترط لكونه مفسداً أمران : أحدهما : أن يكون بلا عذر فإن نشأ من طبعه وكان المصلّي مدفوعاً إليه لا يفسد. وثانيهما : أن يكون لا لغرض صحيح ، فلو كان لتحسين صوته لا يفسد ; لأنه يفعله لإصلاح القراءة، فكان ملحقاً بها، وكذا لو تنحنح لإعلام أنه في الصلاة، أو ليهتدي إمامه إلى الصواب لا يفسد، وهذا كله هو الصحيح.
[4] His statement: 'and clearing the throat' (tanahnuh). It is that he says 'ah, ah' with fatha and dammah, as stated in 'al-Bahr'. The reason for invalidity is that it is speech. Two conditions are required for it to be an invalidator: First, that it is without excuse; if it arises from his natural disposition and the praying person is forced to it, it does not invalidate. Second, that it is not for a valid purpose; if it is to improve his voice (for recitation), it does not invalidate because he does it to correct the recitation, so it is annexed to it. Similarly, if he clears his throat to inform others that he is in prayer, or to guide his imam to the correct (recitation), it does not invalidate. All this is the correct view.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This final passage discusses the ruling on clearing the throat (tanahnuh) during prayer. Linguistically, it means saying 'ah, ah' (with open and closed vowels). The commentator explains that the underlying reason it can invalidate is that it is considered speech (kalam) because it produces letters. However, it does not always invalidate. Two conditions must be met for it to be an invalidator: (1) it must be done without a valid excuse (i.e., not involuntary); and (2) it must not be for a legitimate purpose. If the throat clearing arises from one's natural constitution (like phlegm) and the person is compelled to do it, then it does not invalidate. Also, if the purpose is to improve one's voice for recitation (i.e., to make the recitation better), then it is considered part of the recitation and does not invalidate. Similarly, clearing the throat to signal others that one is in prayer (e.g., to prevent someone from crossing in front) or to guide the imam to correct recitation (like correcting a mistake) is permissible and does not invalidate. This is the relied-upon position in the school. The principle is that actions that are for the benefit of the prayer or unavoidable are exempt from invalidation.

Key Terms
التنحنح (al-tanahnuh): Clearing the throat by producing a sound like 'ah ah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Clearing throat because of a dry throat.
B) Clearing throat to inform others that you are in prayer.
C) Clearing throat out of habit.
D) Clearing throat to attract someone's attention.

Key Takeaway
Clearing the throat invalidates prayer only if it is voluntary and not for a prayer-related benefit; otherwise, the prayer remains valid.

Actions That Nullify Prayer: Groaning, Crying, Clearing Throat, and Responding to Sneezes
Hanafi legal analysis of utterances that nullify prayer, with definitions, conditions, and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for when an utterance during prayer invalidates it and identify valid exceptions based on the text.

Core Idea
Intentional speech in prayer invalidates it, but necessary or involuntary utterances are excused.

Prayer requires focus and abstention from worldly speech. However, not all sounds break the prayer. This lesson analyses the Hanafi rulings on groaning, crying, throat-clearing, and responding to sneezes, distinguishing between invalidating speech and excusable utterances based on evidence from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify each utterance and its default ruling
List the four utterances explicitly mentioned: groaning (al-ta'awwuh), sighing (al-ta'fif), crying (al-buka'), and throat-clearing (al-tanahnuh). Note that each is considered speech (kalam) and thus invalidates by default, unless an exception applies.
2
Understand the underlying principle: speech invalidates
The general principle is that any intentional utterance of two or more letters during prayer invalidates it, unless it is part of the prayer itself or is excused. This is based on the hadith: 'This prayer of ours does not admit any of the speech of people.'
The passage also mentions istirja' and responding to sneezes as additional examples.
3
List the conditions required for invalidation
For an utterance to invalidate, the text requires: (1) it must be without excuse (e.g., not involuntary), (2) it must not be for a valid purpose (e.g., improving recitation), and (3) it must produce discernible letters (at least two).
If the utterance arises from compulsion (like a sick person moaning) or is done for prayer improvement, it does not invalidate.
4
Recognize the exceptions
Key exceptions: crying from piety (remembrance of Paradise and Hell), involuntary actions (sneezing, coughing, yawning), throat-clearing to improve recitation or to signal the imam, and the sneezer praising Allah in prayer. These are excused because they are either not under control or are attached to the prayer.
The text also notes that responding to a sneezer with 'yarhamukallah' invalidates because it is human speech, but the sneezer's own 'al-hamdu lillah' does not.
5
Examine the evidence and the role of istihsan
The Hanafis use istihsan (juristic preference) to depart from strict qiyas in some cases. For example, the hadith of 'Ali shows the Prophet clearing his throat to give permission, so a purposeful throat-clearing is allowed. Similarly, crying from piety is not worldly speech.
This shows that rulings are not purely analogical but consider the intent and context.

And groaning, and saying 'af', and crying aloud from pain or calamity, and clearing the throat without excuse. Groaning: with emphasis on the waw, on the pattern of tafa''ul, that he says 'awh' or 'ah' with elongation. Saying 'af': that he says 'af' or 'tuf'. The reason for nullification of prayer with these things, and likewise with crying, is that what is meant is that letters emerge from it, because it is speech, and it is absolutely invalidating. [1] His saying: 'and crying'; it is with dammah and elongation: sound with tears, and with shortening: tears. Thus in 'al-Sihah'. So his saying 'with sound' is a restriction for the second meaning, and clarification for the first. [2] His saying: 'with sound'; i.e. on condition that two letters or more are produced. As for tears without sound, or sound without letters, it is not invalidating. Thus in 'al-Nahr', 'al-Fath', and 'al-Nihayah'. (3) His saying: 'from pain'; i.e. in his body. 'And calamity'; i.e. in his wealth. This is a restriction for crying, to exclude when the crying is from remembrance of Paradise and Hellfire, for it does not invalidate as he will mention. And it is said: it relates to the four, and an exception is made for a sick person who cannot control himself from groaning or sighing, for it does not invalidate, because then it is like sneezing, coughing, belching, and yawning; for they do not invalidate even if letters are produced, because what is not under choice is excused. [4] His saying: 'and clearing the throat'; it is that he says 'ah ah' with fatha and dammah, said in 'al-Bahr'. Nullification lies in it being speech. For it to be invalidating, two conditions are required: one: that it be without excuse; if it arises from his nature and the worshiper is compelled to it, it does not invalidate. The second: that it be not for a valid purpose; so if it is to improve his voice, it does not invalidate because he does it to correct the recitation, so it is attached to it. Likewise, if he clears his throat to indicate that he is in prayer, or for his imam to be guided to correctness, it does not invalidate. This whole is the correct view. (1) (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 2:5). And responding to a sneezer, and answering bad news with istirja', and other news with hamdalah, and expressing wonder with subhallah and hillalah. By analogy, all are invalidating, except for the compelled one, because it is speech and it is invalidating in any case. But (the Hanafis) departed from analogy due to traditions transmitted to that effect, among them what is in 'Sunan Ibn Majah': from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'I had two ways of entering upon the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ), by night and by day, and when I came to him while he was praying, he would clear his throat.' And the detail of this topic is to be sought in the commentaries on 'al-Munyah': 'al-Ghunyah' and 'al-Halbah'. (1) [1] His saying: 'and responding to a sneezer'; it is with shin and with important letter, the first being more eloquent; it is the response of the hearer to the sneezer's 'al-hamdu lillah' with 'yarhamukallah'. Its addition to the sneezer is addition to the object, and its agent is the worshiper. Meaning, if a man sneezes, whether he be a worshiper or not, and a worshiper says to him 'yarhamukallah', his prayer is invalidated, because it occurs in people's speech, so it becomes speech. (2) This is indicated by the saying of the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ): 'Verily this prayer of ours does not admit any of the speech of people,' said as a teaching to a Companion who responded to a sneezer in prayer, and he forbade him from it as narrated in 'Sunan Abi Dawud' and others. As for if the sneezer says to himself in prayer 'yarhamukallah', or the hearer worshiper says 'al-hamdu lillah', or the sneezer after sneezing in prayer says 'al-hamdu lillah' as is sunnah, his prayer is not invalidated according to the correct view, and in the last there is a difference not considered, as verified by the commentators of 'al-Hidayah' and 'al-Munyah'. [2] His saying: 'and answering'; conjunct to the speech, 'bad news' with kasr: description of news, with 'istirja''. Meaning, if a man informs a worshiper of a calamity such as death and the like, and he says in prayer 'inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un', his prayer is invalidated. (1) Mushkil al-Athar 4:298, al-Mu'tasar min al-Mukhtasar 1:55, Musnad Ahmad 1:81, Musnad al-Bazzar 3:101, and others. (2) Sahih Muslim 1:381, Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah 2:35, and others.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Hanafi madhhab on utterances that nullify prayer, providing definitions, conditions, and exceptions. The author begins by listing four actions: التأوه (groaning), التأفيف (saying 'af' in annoyance), البكاء (crying aloud), and التنحنح (clearing the throat) without excuse. Each is defined: التأوه is saying 'awh' or 'ah' with elongation; التأفيف is saying 'af' or 'tuf'. The underlying reason for nullification is that these produce discernible letters, constituting speech (kalam), and speech intentionally done invalidates prayer absolutely. The commentary clarifies that for crying, the term 'بكاء' here means sound with tears (with madd) or just tears (with qasr); 'بصوت' restricts it to crying that produces at least two letters, so silent tears or sound without letters do not invalidate. The qualifiers 'من وجع' (from physical pain) and 'مصيبة' (from calamity) exclude crying due to remembrance of Paradise or Hell, which does not invalidate. An exception is made for a sick person who cannot control moaning or groaning, likened to involuntary actions like sneezing, coughing, belching, yawning — these do not invalidate even if letters emerge. For clearing the throat (التنحنح), defined as saying 'ah ah', two conditions for invalidation are required: (1) it must be without excuse — if it arises naturally and the worshiper is compelled, it does not invalidate; (2) it must not be for a valid purpose — if done to improve recitation, to signal that one is in prayer, or to guide the imam, it is not invalid because it is attached to the prayer itself. The author then discusses other utterances: تشميت العاطس (responding to a sneezer), جواب خبر سوء بالاسترجاع (answering bad news with 'inna lillahi'), and expressing wonder with التسبيح والتهليل. By strict analogy (qiyas), all these would invalidate because they are speech. However, the Hanafis depart from qiyas due to authentic traditions (آثار). The hadith from Ibn Majah quotes 'Ali saying the Prophet would clear his throat in prayer to signal permission, showing some speech is tolerated. Similarly, the hadith 'إن صلاتنا هذه لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس' from Sahih Muslim serves as evidence against responding to sneezes. But exceptions are detailed: if the sneezer says 'al-hamdu lillah' to himself, or the hearer says 'al-hamdu lillah', or the sneezer praises Allah after sneezing as per sunnah, these do not invalidate on the correct view. The passage underscores that the mufta bihi position relies on istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas, based on specific narrations and the principle that necessary or beneficial speech is excused.

Key Terms
التأوه (al-ta'awwuh): Saying 'awh' or 'ah' with elongation due to pain or distress.
التأفيف (al-ta'fif): Saying 'af' or 'tuf' in annoyance.
البكاء (al-buka'): Crying that produces sound with tears; here restricted to crying with at least two letters.
التنحنح (al-tanahnuh): Clearing the throat by saying 'ah ah'.
تشميت العاطس (tashmit al-'atis): Responding to a sneezer who praised Allah by saying 'yarhamukallah'.
الاسترجاع (al-istirja'): Saying 'inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' upon hearing bad news.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions would NOT invalidate a person's prayer?
A) Crying loudly from physical pain while praying.
B) Clearing the throat to improve recitation during prayer.
C) Saying 'af' in annoyance during prayer.
D) Responding to a sneezer with 'yarhamukallah' during prayer.

Key Takeaway
Intentional speech invalidates prayer, but necessary, involuntary, or prayer-enhancing utterances are excused in the Hanafi school.

When Dhikr Becomes Invalidating Speech in Prayer
Ruling on specific responses that invalidate prayer with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each invalidating utterance (tashmit, istirja', hamdalah, tasbih, tahlil) in prayer and distinguish when they invalidate versus when they do not based on the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
A dhikr-form utterance invalidates prayer if it is a response to an external event.

How can saying 'subhanallah' or 'al-hamdulillah' during prayer break your salah? The key is not the phrase itself but the context: when said as a reply to something happening outside the prayer, these words become 'speech' (kalam) rather than worship. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on five such phrases and the internal dispute between Abu Yusuf and the majority of the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their default ruling
List the five utterances: tashmit (responding 'yarhamukallah'), istirja' ('inna lillah'), hamdalah ('al-hamdulillah'), tasbih ('subhanallah'), tahlil ('la ilaha illallah'). The matn states that saying these in prayer invalidates it. Note the default hukm is batil.
The base rule is that any speech (kalam al-nas) invalidates prayer, based on the hadith.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The text cites the hadith: 'Our prayer is not fit for any of the speech of people.' This is the foundational proof. Determine its thubut (zanni) and dalala (zanni).
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawood and others; it is ahad, hence zanni in transmission.
3
Understand the exception and nuance
The text adds exceptions: if the sneezer says 'yarhamukallah' to himself, or the responder merely says 'lillah', or the sneezer says 'al-hamdulillah' as per sunnah, the prayer is not invalidated. Also, if the person intends to inform that he is in prayer (not to reply), it does not invalidate.
The key distinction is intent: response to an external event vs. mere remembrance or self-address.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify the disagreement: Abu Yusuf holds that these utterances are adhkar (remembrances) and do not invalidate. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that they come out as a response (mukharajat al-jawab) and thus become like conversation.
The dispute is about the illa: is the form (sigha) or the context (response) decisive?
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is the relied-upon position. The commentary states 'wa lahum' (for them) favoring the invalidation stance.
The practical consequence (thamarah): under Abu Yusuf's view, these utterances would be valid; under the majority, they invalidate.

Source Text

كتاب الصلاة
ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها
وتشميت عاطس، وجواب خبر سوء بالاسترجاع ، وسار بالحمدلة، وعجب بالسبحلة والهيللة
قوله : وتشميت عاطس ؛ هو بالشين المعجمة وبالمهملة، والأول أفصح، جواب لسامع حمدلة العاطس بيرحمك الله ، فإضافته إلى العاطس إضافة إلى المفعول، وفاعله المصلي، يعني إذا عطس رجل مصلياً كان أو غيره فقال له مصل: يرحمك الله فسدت صلاته ؛ لأنه يجري في تخاطب الناس فصار كلاماً.
دل عليه قول رسول الله : «إن صلاتنا هذه لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس ، قاله تعليماً لصحابي شَمَّت عاطساً في الصلاة، ومنعه منه كما هو مروي في «سنن أبي داود» وغيره.
وأما إذا قال العاطس لنفسه في الصلاة: يرحمك الله ، أو قال المصلي السامع : لله ، أو قال العاطس بعد العطسة في الصلاة: الحمد لله، على ما هو المسنون لا تفسد صلاته على الصحيح، وفي الأخير خلاف غير معتبر، كما حققه شــراح «الهداية»، وشرّاح «المنية». قوله : وجواب ؛ عطف على الكلام، خبر سوء بالكسر : صفة لخبر بالاسترجاع، يعني إذا أخبر رجل مصليا بمصيبة كموت ونحوه فقال في الصلاة : «إنا لله وإنا اليه راجعون» تفسد صلاته.
وسار عطف على سوء.
عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
بالحمدلة على وزن فعللة بمعنى قول: «الحمد الله» يعني إذا أخبر رجل مصلياً بخبر حصل له منه السرور فقال : شكراً، أو جواباً الحمد لله تفسد صلاته.
وعجيب عطف على سوء. بالسبحلة أي قول : سبحان الله.
والهيللة أي قول: لا إله إلا الله ، يعني إذا أخبر المصلّي رجل بخبر حصل له منه تعجب فقال : سبحان الله، أو لا إله إلا الله، تفسد صلاته في هذه المسائل خلاف أبي يوسف، فإنّه يقول : هذه أذكار بصيغتها، فلا تفسدها.
ولهما : أَنها لَمَّا خَرَجَت مخرج الجواب، وهي محتملة له تجعل جواباً، فيكون كالتشميت، نعم لو لم يرد الجواب، بل أراد إعلام أنه في الصلاة لم يفسد. كذا في «شروح الهداية».
Book of Prayer: What invalidates prayer and what is disliked in it. And responding to a sneezer (tashmit), and answering bad news with istirja' (saying inna lillah), and (answering) good news with hamdalah (saying al-hamdulillah), and (answering) something amazing with tasbih (saying subhanallah) and tahlil (saying la ilaha illallah). His saying: 'And tashmit of a sneezer' – it is with the dotted shin and with the undotted sin, the first is more eloquent. It is the response of the hearer to the sneezer's hamdalah with 'yarhamukallah'. Its attribution to the sneezer is attribution to the object, and its doer is the praying person. It means: if a man sneezes, whether he is praying or not, and a person praying says to him 'yarhamukallah', his prayer becomes invalid because it is in the conversation of people, so it becomes speech. This is indicated by the saying of the Messenger of Allah: 'This prayer of ours is not fit for any of the speech of people.' He said this to teach a companion who responded to a sneezer in prayer and forbade him from it, as narrated in Sunan Abi Dawood and others. As for if the sneezer says to himself in prayer 'yarhamukallah', or the hearing person praying says 'lillah', or the sneezer says after sneezing in prayer 'al-hamdulillah', according to what is sunnah, his prayer is not invalidated according to the correct view. And regarding the last, there is a disagreement not considered, as verified by the commentators of al-Hidayah and al-Munya. His saying: 'And answering' – is conjoined to 'speech'. 'Bad news' with kasra: attribute of 'news'. With istirja' means: if a man informs a person praying of a calamity like death and the like, and he says in prayer 'inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un', his prayer becomes invalid. 'And other' is conjoined to 'bad'. [Placeholder text] 'With hamdalah' on the pattern of fa'lalah, meaning saying 'al-hamdulillah' – it means if a man informs a person praying of news from which he derives pleasure, and he says 'shukran' or in answer 'al-hamdulillah', his prayer becomes invalid. 'And something amazing' is conjoined to 'bad'. 'With tasbih' meaning saying 'subhanallah'. 'And tahlil' meaning saying 'la ilaha illallah' – it means if a man informs a person praying of news from which he derives amazement, and he says 'subhanallah' or 'la ilaha illallah', his prayer becomes invalid. In these matters there is a disagreement with Abu Yusuf, for he says: these are remembrances (adhkar) in their form, so they do not invalidate it. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): since they come out as a response and are probable to be a response, they are made a response, so they are like tashmit. Yes, if he did not intend the response, but rather intended to inform that he is in prayer, it does not invalidate. Thus in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage rules on specific utterances that invalidate prayer. It is a ruling-with-proof passage with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn lists: tashmit (responding to a sneezer with 'yarhamukallah'), answering bad news with istirja' ('inna lillah'), answering good news with hamdalah ('al-hamdulillah'), and answering something amazing with tasbih ('subhanallah') or tahlil ('la ilaha illallah'). The commentator explains that these are speech that imitates everyday conversation, thus invalidating prayer, supported by the hadith 'our prayer is not fit for any speech of people'. An exception is when the sneezer praises Allah silently or the responder merely says 'lillah' without intending reply; these do not invalidate. The commentator also notes that Abu Yusuf holds that these phrases are dhikr (remembrance) and do not invalidate because they are inherently forms of worship. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that the phrases become invalidating because they are used as responses to external events, making them like ordinary speech. However, if the person only intends to inform that he is in prayer (not to reply), the prayer remains valid.

Key Terms
تشميت (tashmit): Responding to a sneezer by saying 'yarhamukallah' (may Allah have mercy on you).
استرجاع (istirja'): Saying 'inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi raji'un' upon hearing bad news.
حمدلة (hamdalah): Saying 'al-hamdulillah' (praise be to Allah).
سبحلة (sabhala/tasbih): Saying 'subhanallah' (glory be to Allah).
هيللة (haylala/tahlil): Saying 'la ilaha illallah' (there is no god but Allah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The prayer is valid because it is dhikr.
B) The prayer is invalidated and must be repeated.
C) The prayer becomes makruh but remains valid.
D) The prayer is nullified only if he intended to reply.

Key Takeaway
Uttering dhikr as a response to external events invalidates prayer because it becomes kalam, per the majority Hanafi view; intention to respond is the key factor.

Prompting the Imam: Permissibility and Limits
Explains the permissibility and limits of prompting the imam during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prompting the imam during prayer and identify the conditions under which it is permitted.

Core Idea
Prompting the imam is permitted out of necessity to avoid hardship, for both obligatory and supererogatory prayers.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling on interaction during prayer: the permissibility of correcting or prompting the imam (fatḥ). While the general rule prohibits speaking in prayer, necessity arising from human forgetfulness allows an exception for the imam. We will explore the textual proof and the scope of this concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is prompting the imam (al-fatḥ 'ala al-imam). The ruling is that it is permitted out of necessity (mubah li al-darura).
The passage uses the phrase 'juzzia darura' indicating permissibility due to necessity.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith in Sunan Abi Dawud showing the Prophet approved being reminded of a missed verse.
The hadith is cited partially; the full text includes the Prophet's statement 'halā adhkartanīhā'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's approval (taqrir) of the companion's reminder indicates permissibility. Additionally, the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj) is invoked.
Forgetfulness is natural; prohibition would cause undue difficulty.
4
Note the scope
The permission applies to both fard and nafl prayers, including tarawih.
The text explicitly says 'sawā'a kāna dhālika fī ṣalāt al-farḍ aw al-nafl'.
5
Evaluate the limitation
The phrase 'on other than his imam' implies that prompting a non-imam (e.g., another worshipper) is not similarly permitted.
This restricts the concession to the imam alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وفتحه على غير إمامه ؛ اعلم أن الفتح على إمامه، وتلقينه عند احتياجه إليه جُوِّز ضرورة، فإنّ السهو والنسيان غالب على الإنسان، فلو لم يجز لأدّى ذلك إلى الحرج، سواء كان ذلك في صلاة الفرض أو النفل كالتراويح وغيره ؛ لما في «سنن أبي داود» عن ابن عمر : «صلى النبي صلاة فلبس عليه، فلما فرغ قال لأبي ابن كعب : أشهدت الصلاة معنا ، قال : نعم؟ قال : فما منعك» وفيه أيضاً عن المسور بن مخرمة : أن رسول الله «كان يقرأ في الصلاة فترك شيئاً لم يقرأه، فقال له رجل: يا رسول الله تركت آية كذا وكذا، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : هلا أذكرتنيها ، قال : كنت أراها نسخت»
His saying: 'And his prompting to other than his imam' – know that prompting (al-fatḥ) to one's imam and cueing him when he needs it is permitted out of necessity, for forgetfulness and oversight are dominant in man. If it were not permitted, it would lead to hardship, whether this is in obligatory prayer or supererogatory like tarawih and other. Because in Sunan Abi Dawood from Ibn 'Umar: 'The Prophet prayed a prayer and became confused. When he finished he said to Ubayy ibn Ka'b: 'Were you present with us for the prayer?' He said: 'Yes.' He said: 'What prevented you?' And in it also from al-Miswar ibn Makhrama that the Messenger of Allah was reciting in prayer and left out something he did not recite, so a man said to him: 'O Messenger of Allah, you left out such-and-such a verse,' so the Messenger of Allah said: 'Why did you not remind me of it?' He said: 'I thought it was abrogated.'
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage rules on prompting the imam (fatḥ) during prayer. It is a ruling-with-proof passage. The matn states 'wa fatḥihi 'ala ghayri imamihi' (and his prompting to other than his imam). The commentator explains that prompting the imam, i.e., correcting or reminding him of verses, is permitted out of necessity because forgetfulness is common. If it were not allowed, it would cause hardship. This applies to both fard and nafl prayers, including tarawih. The proof is a hadith from Sunan Abi Dawud where the Prophet allowed a companion to remind him of a missed verse. However, prompting a person other than the imam (e.g., another worshipper) is not similarly permitted and would invalidate the prayer, as implied by the phrase 'on other than his imam'.

Key Terms
الفتح على الإمام (al-fatḥ 'ala al-imam): Prompting or cueing the imam during prayer to correct a mistake or remind him of a verse.
تلقين (talqin): Synonym for fatḥ, meaning to prompt or cue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Prompting the imam during prayer is permitted out of necessity to avoid hardship, based on prophetic practice and the principle of removing difficulty.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Dhikr as Response in Prayer
Explains that saying الحمد لله etc. as response invalidates prayer with Abu Yusuf's disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dhikr phrases said as responses in prayer and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Dhikr as a response becomes speech — the context of utterance determines the ruling, not the literal wording.

This lesson addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: when a person praying says phrases like 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' or 'subḥāna llāh' in response to external news, does this invalidate the prayer? While these are normally acts of dhikr, the context of response changes their legal nature. We will examine the majority view and Abu Yusuf's dissent, and the basis for each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the action? Saying specific dhikr phrases in prayer as a response to external news. What is the ruling per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad? It invalidates.
2
Locate the disagreement
Who differs? Abu Yusuf. What does he hold? Prayer is not invalidated because these are dhikr.
3
Examine the proofs
What is the proof of the majority? The utterances come out as a response, so they are treated as speech like tashmīt. What is Abu Yusuf's proof? The wording is dhikr, so it remains dhikr.
4
Analyze the response
How do the majority rebut? The possibility of being a response overrides the literal form; intent is inferred from context.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Which position is relied upon? The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسار عطف على سوء. بالحمدلة على وزن فعللة بمعنى قول: «الحمد الله» يعني إذا أخبر رجل مصلياً بخبر حصل له منه السرور فقال : شكراً، أو جواباً الحمد لله تفسد صلاته. وعجيب عطف على سوء. بالسبحلة أي قول : سبحان الله. والهيللة أي قول: لا إله إلا الله ، يعني إذا أخبر المصلّي رجل بخبر حصل له منه تعجب فقال : سبحان الله، أو لا إله إلا الله، تفسد صلاته في هذه المسائل خلاف أبي يوسف، فإنّه يقول : هذه أذكار بصيغتها، فلا تفسدها. ولهما : أَنها لَمَّا خَرَجَت مخرج الجواب، وهي محتملة له تجعل جواباً، فيكون كالتشميت، نعم لو لم يرد الجواب، بل أراد إعلام أنه في الصلاة لم يفسد. كذا في «شروح الهداية».
And the rest is an annex to 'sū''. 'Al-ḥamdala' on the measure of fa'lala meaning the saying 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'. That is, if a person informs a praying man of news that gives him delight, and he says 'shukran' or in answer 'al-ḥamdu lillāh', his prayer is invalidated. And 'al-ʿajab' is an annex to 'sū''. 'Al-sabaḥala' meaning the saying 'subḥāna llāh'. And 'al-haylala' meaning the saying 'lā ilāha illā llāh'. That is, if a praying person is informed by a man of news that causes him wonder and he says 'subḥāna llāh' or 'lā ilāha illā llāh', his prayer is invalidated. In these matters there is disagreement with Abū Yūsuf, for he says: these are remembrances in their form, so they do not invalidate. And for the two (Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad): that when they come out as a response, and they are possible for that, they are made a response, so it is like 'tashmīt'. Yes, if he did not intend the response but intended to inform that he is in prayer, it does not invalidate. Thus in 'Shurūḥ al-Hidāyah'.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on utterances that are normally dhikr but when said as a response to external news become speech that invalidates prayer. The author defines three terms: الحمدلة (saying الحمد لله), السبحلة (saying سبحان الله), and الهيللة (saying لا إله إلا الله). He states that if a praying person says these phrases in response to news (joy or wonder), the prayer is invalidated according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Yusuf disagrees, saying these are a form of dhikr in their wording, so they do not invalidate. The proof for the majority is that because these utterances came out as a response, they are treated as speech, similar to التشميت (saying يرحمك الله in response to a sneeze). However, if the person did not intend to respond but intended to inform that he is in prayer (e.g., by raising his voice to indicate he is in prayer), it does not invalidate. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الحمدلة (al-ḥamdala): The verbal noun meaning the utterance of 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'.
السبحلة (al-sabaḥala): The verbal noun meaning the utterance of 'subḥāna llāh'.
الهيللة (al-haylala): The verbal noun meaning the utterance of 'lā ilāha illā llāh'.
التشميت (al-tashmīt): The act of saying 'yarḥamuka llāh' to a sneezer. It is considered speech that invalidates prayer if said intentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the effect on prayer if a praying person says 'subḥāna llāh' in response to news that causes wonder?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid because it is a dhikr.
C) The prayer is valid only if said silently.
D) The prayer is invalid only if said deliberately.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, dhikr phrases said as responses in prayer are treated as speech and invalidate the prayer, even though the same words are normally permissible.
