Ruling on Prompting in Prayer: Allowed for Imam, Invalidating for Others
The passage presents the Hanafi ruling on prompting in prayer with evidence and an intra-madhhab ikhtilāf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prompting in prayer, distinguish between prompting the imam and prompting others, and apply the exception of necessity.

Core Idea
Prompting anyone other than your own imam invalidates prayer; prompting your imam is permitted out of necessity.

In this lesson, we examine the legal ruling on prompting (al-fatḥ) in prayer. The Hanafi school generally considers prompting as akin to speech, which breaks the prayer. However, an exception is made for prompting the imam when he forgets, based on the necessity of preventing hardship. The passage provides evidence from the Sunnah and discusses a later scholarly dispute about the limits of this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The passage states that prompting anyone other than one's own imam invalidates the prayer. This includes prompting another follower, a solitary person, a different imam, etc.
The term 'fatḥ' covers both prompting and coaching.
2
Understand the exception
Prompting the imam is permitted when needed due to forgetfulness, which is common. This exception is based on necessity (ḍarūrah) to avoid hardship.
The exception applies whether the prayer is obligatory or supererogatory.
3
Examine the evidence
The author cites hadith from Sunan Abī Dāwūd where the Prophet approved of being reminded during prayer, and also the saying of ʿAlī 'if your imam asks you to feed him, then feed him'.
Thus, the evidence supports the permissibility of prompting the imam.
4
Analyze the later ikhtilāf
Later mashāyikh differed on whether prompting the imam invalidates if the imam has already recited the minimum required (miqdār mā tajūzu bih al-ṣalāh) or moved to another verse.
The correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that it does not invalidate, based on the hadith where the Prophet encouraged a man to remind him despite having recited enough.
5
Determine the muftā bihī
The relied-upon position is that prompting the imam is permissible and does not invalidate, even if the imam has fulfilled the minimum recitation. Prompting others remains invalidating.
This ruling is from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah and supported by the primary evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وفتحه على غير إمامه؛ اعلم أن الفتح على إمامه، وتلقينه عند احتياجه إليه جُوِّز ضرورة، فإنّ السهو والنسيان غالب على الإنسان، فلو لم يجز لأدّى ذلك إلى الحرج، سواء كان ذلك في صلاة الفرض أو النفل كالتراويح وغيره ؛ لما في «سنن أبي داود» عن ابن عمر له : «صلى النبي صلاة فلبس عليه، فلما فرغ قال لأبي ابن كعب : أشهدت الصلاة معنا ، قال : نعم؟ قال : فما منعك» وفيه أيضاً عن المسور له أن رسول الله : «كان يقرأ في الصلاة فترك شيئاً لم يقرأه، فقال له رجل: يا رسول الله تركت آية كذا وكذا، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : هلا أذكر تنيها ، قال : كنت أراها نسخت». وعن علي الله قال : «إذا استطعمك الإمام فأطعمه». وإنما قال : على غير إمامه ؛ لأنَّ فتحه على إمامه لا يفسد. وأما الفتح على غيره إمامه فمفسد ، وهو شامل لصور أن يفتح المقتدي على مثله، وأن يفتح المقتدي على المنفرد، وأن يفتح على غيره المصلي وأن يفتح على إمام آخر، وأن يفتح الإمام أو المنفرد على أي شخص كان كذا في «النهر». وجه الفساد : إن الفتح تعليم وتلقين، فأشبه الكلام، وإنّما جُوِّزَ على إمامه للضرورة، فيبقى في غير مواضعها مفسداً وتفسد صلاة المستفتح المصلّي أيضاً بأخذه من الخارج ؛ لوجود التعلم من الخارج. وتفصيل هذه المباحث والمسائل مع ما لها وما عليها في رسالتي : «قوت المغتذين بفتح المقتدين»، وفي رسالتي القول الأشرف في الفتح عن المصحف». واختلف المشايخ فيما إذا قرأ الإمامُ مقدار ما تجوزُ به الصلاة أو انتقل إلى آية أخرى ، هل تفسد صلاة الإمام إن أخذ فتح المؤتم، وصلاةُ المؤتم بفتحه؟ وكذا اختلف فيما إذا قرأ الإمامُ مقدار ما تجوز به الصلاة وتوقف ولم ينتقل إلى آية أخرى ففتحه المقتدي هل تفسد ؟ فقال بعضهم : نعم ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الفتح إنّما جُوِّزَ للضرورة، ولا ضرورة في هذه الصورة. والأصح عدم الفساد مطلقاً، يدلّ عليه حديث أبي داود المار ذكره، فإنّ النبي لما ترك آية قال : «الرجل أهلا أذكر تنيها»، فلو لم يكن مطلق الفتح جائزاً لما حضضه على الفتح مع قراءة قدر ما تجوز به الصلاة.
His saying: 'and his prompting (فتح) of other than his imam'. Know that prompting the imam and coaching him when he needs it is permitted out of necessity, for forgetfulness and slip of memory are prevalent in humans. If it were not permitted, it would lead to hardship. This applies whether in obligatory or supererogatory prayers such as tarāwīḥ and others. For there is in Sunan Abī Dāwūd from Ibn 'Umar: 'The Prophet prayed a prayer and became confused. When he finished, he said to Ubayy ibn Ka'b: Did you witness the prayer with us? He said: Yes. He said: So what prevented you?' And also from al-Miswar that the Messenger of Allah was reciting in prayer and left out something. A man said: O Messenger of Allah, you left out such and such verse. The Messenger of Allah said: Why did you not remind me? He said: I thought it was abrogated.' And from 'Alī: 'If your imam asks you to feed him (i.e., prompts you), then feed him.' He only said 'other than his imam' because prompting his imam does not invalidate. As for prompting other than his imam, it is invalidating. This includes cases: a follower prompting another follower, a follower prompting a person praying alone, a person in prayer prompting another person in prayer, a follower prompting another imam, and an imam or a solitary person prompting any person. Thus in 'al-Nahr'. The reason for invalidation: prompting is teaching and coaching, so it resembles speech. It was only permitted for the imam out of necessity, so it remains invalidating in other situations. And the prayer of the one who is prompted (the praying person who receives the prompt) is also invalidated by taking (the recitation) from outside, because there is learning from outside. The details of these discussions and rulings with their arguments are in my treatise 'Qūt al-Mughtadhīn bi-Fatḥ al-Muqtadīn' and my treatise 'al-Qawl al-Ashraf fī al-Fatḥ 'an al-Muṣḥaf'. The mashāyikh differed concerning cases where the imam recited the amount sufficient for the prayer or moved to another verse: does the prayer of the imam become invalid if he accepts the follower's prompt, and does the follower's prayer become invalid due to his prompting? Similarly, they differed concerning the case where the imam recited the amount sufficient for the prayer but paused and did not move to another verse, and the follower prompted him: does it invalidate? Some said yes, because prompting is only permitted out of necessity, and there is no necessity in this case. The correct view is that it does not invalidate absolutely, as indicated by the hadith of Abū Dāwūd mentioned earlier, for the Prophet left out a verse and said: 'The man: why did you not remind me?' If prompting in the absolute sense were not permissible, he would not have urged him to prompt while he had already recited the amount sufficient for the prayer.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on prompting (فتح/تلقين) in prayer. The term 'فتح' refers to supplying a missed word or verse to someone who is reciting. The general rule is that prompting in prayer is akin to speech and therefore invalidates the prayer, except when the one prompted is the imam and he is in need. This exception is based on necessity (ضرورة) because forgetfulness is human, and disallowing it would cause hardship. The evidence is from the practice of the Companions who prompted the Prophet during prayer, and he approved it. The author specifies 'on other than his imam' because prompting the imam does not invalidate. Prompting any other person (another follower, a solitary person, a different imam, etc.) invalidates the prayer of both the prompter and the one who receives the prompt and acts upon it. Later scholars (المشايخ) discussed details: if the imam has already completed the minimum required recitation (مقدار ما تجوز به الصلاة) or has moved to another verse, does prompting still invalidate? The correct view (الأصح) is that it does not invalidate because the Prophet himself encouraged a man to remind him even after reciting enough, showing that absolute necessity is not a condition. The author mentions his own treatises for further details.

Key Terms
الفتح (al-fatḥ): In the context of prayer, it means prompting or supplying a missed word or verse to someone reciting.
التلقين (al-talqīn): Synonymous with فتح; verbally prompting someone in prayer.
مقدار ما تجوز به الصلاة (miqdār mā tajūzu bih al-ṣalāh): The minimum amount of Quranic recitation that suffices for the validity of prayer, which is the length of one short verse or its equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a form of speech.
B) It disturbs concentration.
C) It changes the meaning of the recitation.
D) It is an innovation in worship.

Key Takeaway
Prompting others in prayer invalidates because it is like speech; prompting the imam is exempted due to necessity.

Three Categories of Speech in Prayer
Classifies speech in prayer into three types and states their effect on validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any utterance during prayer into one of three Hanafi categories and state whether it invalidates the prayer or requires sajdat al-sahw.

Core Idea
Speech in prayer is either invalidating, harmless, or disputed — only ordinary conversation always breaks the prayer.

In this lesson, we examine a concise but critical classification from the Hanafi school that governs every utterance made during prayer. Understanding which type of speech invalids the prayer and which merely requires a prostration of forgetfulness is essential for every Muslim. The text presents three categories with clear rulings, including a notable internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the third type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the Arabic text and locate the three types numbered 1, 2, and 3. Note the defining feature of each: wording (عين) and meaning (معنى) relative to ordinary human speech.
Type 1: both wording and meaning are speech; Type 2: neither wording nor meaning is speech; Type 3: wording is dhikr but meaning is speech.
2
Understand the ruling for Type 1 (agreed)
Type 1 speech invalidates the prayer by consensus (اتفاقاً) whether little or much, intentional or forgetful. This is the absolute case.
Examples: saying 'give me water' or any conversational phrase.
3
Analyze Type 2: dhikr in the wrong place
Type 2 speech (e.g., Quranic recitation in rukuʿ or sujood) does not invalidate prayer because its wording and meaning are dhikr, not human conversation. However, it requires sajdat al-sahw because it alters the proper place of recitation.
The prayer remains valid; sahwu sajdat al-sahw is wajib.
4
Examine Type 3: the disputed category
Type 3 speech has the wording of dhikr (e.g., 'alhamdulillah') but the meaning of conversation (e.g., responding to a sneeze). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that it invalidates, while Abu Yusuf says it does not.
The mufta bihi is the view of the two (invalidates).
5
Apply the classification
Given a scenario, identify which category the utterance falls into and state the ruling. Remember to consider intention: if the utterance is clearly dhikr but meant as conversation, it is Type 3.
This parsing skill is crucial for correct prayer practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الكلام على ثلاثة أقسام : ١ . ما يكون عينه ومعناه كلاهما، وهذا القسم يفسد الصلاة اتفاقاً بين أئمتنا، وإن قل عمداً كان أو سهواً. ٢. وما لا يكون عينه ولا معناه خطاباً وكلاماً ، وهذا القسم لا يفسد الصلاة اتفاقاً ولو وقع في غير محله، كما إذا قرأ في الركوع والسجودِ أو في التشهد، نعم إن فعل ذلك تجب سجدة السهو. ٣. وما يكون عينه ذكراً ومعناه كلاماً بأن خرج مخرج الجواب والتعليم، وفيه خلاف : فعندهما يفسد ، وعند أبي يوسف له لا يفسد .
Then know that speech (in prayer) is of three types: 1. What is both in its wording and meaning (i.e., human speech), and this type invalidates prayer by agreement among our imams, whether little, intentional, or forgetful. 2. What is neither in its wording nor meaning speech and address (i.e., purely dhikr like Quranic recitation in prayer), and this type does not invalidate prayer by agreement, even if it occurs in an inappropriate place, such as reciting (Quran) during rukūʿ, sujūd, or tashahhud. However, if he does that, sajdat al-sahw becomes obligatory. 3. What is in its wording dhikr but in its meaning speech, such as when it comes out as a response or teaching, and this type is subject to disagreement: according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) it invalidates, and according to Abu Yusuf it does not invalidate.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This concluding passage provides a comprehensive classification of speech (الكلام) in prayer according to the Hanafi school. The author divides speech into three categories. First: speech that is both in wording and meaning — i.e., conventional human speech used for conversation. This type always invalidates prayer, regardless of whether it is a little or much, intentional or forgetful, according to all Hanafi imams. Second: speech that is neither in wording nor meaning conversational speech — this refers to the Quranic recitation or other dhikr that is proper to prayer. Such utterances do not invalidate prayer even if recited in postures where they are not prescribed (e.g., reciting Quran in ruku or sujood). However, by doing so in an incorrect place, the prayer becomes complete but requires sajdat al-sahw because of an alteration in the proper form. Third: an intermediate category where the wording is dhikr (like الحمد لله) but the meaning is conversational speech because it is uttered as a response or teaching. This category is disputed: the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule that it invalidates prayer, while Abu Yusuf maintains it does not because the wording is dhikr.

Key Terms
الكلام (al-kalām): Speech or utterance. In the context of prayer, it refers to any utterance that may or may not invalidate the prayer.
سجدة السهو (sajdat al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness, required to compensate for certain omissions or additions in prayer that do not invalidate it.
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The testimony of faith recited in the sitting posture of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what type of speech invalidates prayer by consensus (اتفاقاً)?
A) Speech that is dhikr in wording and meaning (e.g., Quran recitation in qiyam).
B) Speech that is neither in wording nor meaning conversational (e.g., Quran recitation in rukūʿ).
C) Speech that is both in wording and meaning conversational (e.g., saying 'give me water').
D) Speech that is dhikr in wording but meaning conversational (e.g., saying 'subḥāna llāh' as a response).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies speech in prayer into three categories: ordinary conversation (invalidates), dhikr (valid but may require sajdat al-sahw if misplaced), and ambiguous dhikr-speech (invalidates per majority).

Ruling on Prompting and Three Categories of Speech in Prayer
Explains ruling on al-fath and categorizes speech in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on al-fath in prayer, identify the three categories of speech, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the third category.

Core Idea
Prompting only your imam is allowed; otherwise itnullifies prayer. Speech in prayer has three categories: two agreed upon, one disputed between Sahibayn and Abu Yusuf.

In this passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, the author explains when prompting (al-fath) in prayer invalidates the prayer and when it does not. He then presents the well-known Hanafi classification of speech into three types, highlighting a disagreement within the school about the third type — words that are dhikr in form but carry a communicative intent. We will work through the ruling on prompting, then dissect the three categories and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on al-fath
Identify that prompting one's own imam does not nullify prayer due to necessity, but prompting anyone else does.
Includes prompting a fellow follower, a solitary person, or another imam.
2
Analyze the three categories of speech
List the three types: (1) speech in wording and meaning, (2) neither wording nor meaning as address, (3) dhikr in wording but speech in meaning.
Types 1 and 2 are agreed upon; type 3 is disputed.
3
Examine the dispute on type 3
Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that such utterances invalidate prayer because they are intended as response or teaching; Abu Yusuf holds they do not because the words themselves are dhikr.
The source text says 'according to them (عندهما) it invalidates, and according to Abu Yusuf it does not.'
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Give practical examples: saying 'Jalla Jalaluhu' upon hearing Allah's name, responding to a sneeze, etc. The prayer is invalid according to Sahibayn, valid according to Abu Yusuf.
These examples illustrate the real-world impact of the dispute.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is that of Sahibayn, as authenticated by later scholars like al-Ayni.
The fatwa is given according to the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها
وإنما قال : : على غير إمامه ؛ لأنَّ فتحه على إمامه لا يفسد.
وأما الفتح على غيره إمامه فمفسد ، وهو شامل لصور أن يفتح المقتدي على مثله، وأن يفتح المقتدي على المنفرد، وأن يفتح على غيره المصلي وأن يفتح على إمام آخر، وأن يفتح الإمام أو المنفرد على أي شخص كان كذا في «النهر».
وجه الفساد : إن الفتح تعليم وتلقين، فأشبه الكلام، وإنّما جُوِّزَ على إمامه للضرورة، فيبقى في غير مواضعها مفسداً وتفسد صلاة المستفتح المصلّي أيضاً بأخذه من الخارج ؛ لوجود التعلم من الخارج.
وتفصيل هذه المباحث والمسائل مع ما لها وما عليها في رسالتي : «قوت المغتذين بفتح المقتدين»، وفي رسالتي القول الأشرف في الفتح عن المصحف». واختلف المشايخ فيما إذا قرأ الإمامُ مقدار ما تجوز به الصلاة أو انتقل إلى آية أخرى ، هل تفسد صلاة الإمام إن أخذ فتح المؤتم، وصلاةُ المؤتم بفتحه؟ وكذا اختلف فيما إذا قرأ الإمامُ مقدار ما تجوز به الصلاة وتوقف ولم ينتقل إلى آية أخرى ففتحه المقتدي هل تفسد ؟ فقال بعضهم : نعم ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الفتح إنّما جُوِّزَ للضرورة، ولا ضرورة في هذه، الصورة والأصح عدم الفساد مطلقاً، يدلّ عليه حديث أبي داود المار ذكره، فإنّ النبي لما ترك آية قال : «الرجل أهلا أذكر تنيها»، فلو لم يكن مطلق الفتح جائزاً لما حضضه على الفتح مع قراءة قدر ما تجوز به الصلاة. ثم اعلم أن الكلام على ثلاثة أقسام :
١ . ما يكون عينه ومعناه كلاهما، وهذا القسم يفسد الصلاة اتفاقاً بين أئمتنا، وإن قل عمداً كان أو سهواً. ٢. وما لا يكون عينه ولا معناه خطاباً وكلاماً ، وهذا القسم لا يفسد الصلاة اتفاقاً ولو وقع في غير محله، كما إذا قرأ في الركوع والسجودِ أو في التشهد، نعم إن فعل ذلك تجب سجدة السهو. ٣. وما يكون عينه ذكراً ومعناه كلاماً بأن خرج مخرج الجواب والتعليم، وفيه خلاف : فعندهما يفسد ، وعند أبي يوسف له لا يفسد .
Book of Prayer: What invalidates prayer and what is disliked therein. And he said: 'to other than his imam' because prompting his imam does not invalidate. As for prompting other than his imam, it invalidates, and it includes scenarios: a follower prompting his like, a follower prompting a solitary person, a person prompting another who is praying, prompting another imam, and an imam or solitary person prompting any person, as in al-Nahr. The reason for invalidation: prompting is teaching and coaching, so it resembles speech. It was permitted only for one's imam due to necessity, so it remains invalid in other contexts. And it also invalidates the prayer of the prompted person who takes from an outsider because of learning from an outsider. The details of these discussions and issues with their pros and cons are in my treatises: Qut al-Mughtadhin bi-Fath al-Muqtadin and al-Qawl al-Ashraf fi al-Fath 'an al-Mushaf. The scholars differed regarding when the imam recited an amount sufficient for the prayer or moved to another verse: does the imam's prayer invalidate if he accepts the follower's prompt, and does the follower's prayer invalidate by his prompt? Similarly they differed when the imam recited enough and paused without moving to another verse and the follower prompted him: does it invalidate? Some said yes, because prompting was only permitted for necessity, and there is no necessity in this case. The correct view is that it does not invalidate absolutely, as indicated by the hadith of Abu Dawud mentioned earlier, when the Prophet (peace be upon him) left out a verse and said: 'O man, did you remind me?' If absolute prompting were not permitted, he would not have urged him to prompt while he had recited enough to make the prayer valid. Then know that speech (in prayer) is of three types: 1. That which is both in wording and meaning — this invalidates prayer by consensus among our imams, even if little, whether intentional or forgetful. 2. That which is neither wording nor meaning as address and speech — this does not invalidate prayer by consensus, even if occurring in an improper place, like reciting in ruku, sujud, or tashahhud; however, if one does that, sajdah sahw becomes obligatory. 3. That which is dhikr in wording but speech in meaning, i.e., it comes in the form of a response or teaching — there is a disagreement: according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) it invalidates, and according to Abu Yusuf it does not invalidate.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya deals with the chapter on prayer, specifically what nullifies it and what is disliked. The author begins by explaining the ruling on prompting (al-fath) during prayer. Prompting one's own imam (e.g., correcting his recitation) does not nullify the prayer due to necessity. However, prompting anyone else—whether a fellow follower, a person praying alone, another imam, or any other person—does nullify the prayer. The reasoning is that prompting constitutes teaching and coaching, which resembles speech. It is only permitted for the imam due to need, so it remains invalidating in other contexts. The author then mentions that the details of these rulings are in his other works. Then he discusses a disagreement among scholars regarding a scenario where the imam has recited enough to make the prayer valid and then pauses or moves to another verse: does the follower's prompting nullify the imam's prayer? Some say yes because there is no necessity, but the correct view is that it does not nullify absolutely, supported by the hadith of Abu Dawud where the Prophet (pbuh) prompted a man who forgot a verse, even though he had recited enough. Then the author presents the well-known classification of speech in prayer into three categories: (1) Speech that is both in wording and meaning – this nullifies prayer by consensus, whether intentional or forgetful, even if little. (2) Speech that is neither in wording nor meaning – it does not nullify prayer by consensus, e.g., reciting Quran in ruku or sujud; however, if done deliberately, sajdah sahw is required. (3) Speech where the wording is dhikr but the meaning is speech, i.e., it is said as a response or teaching – here there is a dispute: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), it nullifies prayer; according to Abu Yusuf, it does not. This classification is crucial for understanding what constitutes invalidating speech. Note that the disagreement is only about this third category; the first two categories are agreed upon.

Key Terms
الفتح (al-fath): Prompting or reminding someone during prayer, specifically correcting or supplying a verse of Quran being recited.
الكلام (al-kalam): Speech that is not Quran, dhikr, or salawat; it nullifies prayer if uttered deliberately.
الذكر (al-dhikr): Remembrance of Allah, including tasbih, tahmid, tahlil, that are not intended as human communication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does prompting a person who is praying alone (munfarid) nullify the prayer?
A) Yes, because it constitutes teaching and resembles speech.
B) No, because the words are Quranic and are not speech.
C) Only if the prompt is about a verse of Quran, not if it is about other matters.
D) Yes, because the person praying alone is not an imam.

Key Takeaway
Speech in prayer is categorized into three types; only type 1 and type 3 (according to Sahibayn) invalidate; prompting is allowed only for one's own imam due to necessity.
